<a href="/wiki/File:P_christianity.svg" class="image" title="Christian cross"><img alt="Christian cross" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/20px-P_christianity.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="18" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/30px-P_christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/40px-P_christianity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="360" /></a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Portal:Christianity" title="Portal:Christianity">Christianity portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template talk:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Christianity_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Thomas Aquinas</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/…ô/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">…ô</span><span title="/Àà/: primary stress follows">Àà</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="&#39;w&#39; in &#39;wind&#39;">w</span><span title="/a…™/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;tide&#39;">a…™</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/…ô/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">…ô</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <i lang="it">Tommaso d'Aquino</i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a>&#160;</small>&#39;Thomas of <a href="/wiki/Aquino,_Italy" title="Aquino, Italy">Aquino</a>&#39;; 1225 ‚Äì 7 March 1274) was an Italian<sup id="cite_ref-JPC_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JPC-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RBV_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RBV-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican friar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosopher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catholic_priest" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic priest">Catholic priest</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_the_Church" title="Doctor of the Church">Doctor of the Church</a>. An immensely influential <a href="/wiki/List_of_Catholic_philosophers_and_theologians" title="List of Catholic philosophers and theologians">philosopher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catholic_theology" title="Catholic theology">theologian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jurist" title="Jurist">jurist</a> in the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholasticism</a>, he is also known within the latter as the <i><b>Doctor Angelicus</b></i> and the <i><b>Doctor Communis</b></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The name <i>Aquinas</i> identifies his ancestral origins in the county of Aquino in present-day <a href="/wiki/Lazio" title="Lazio">Lazio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>. He was the foremost classical proponent of <a href="/wiki/Natural_theology" title="Natural theology">natural theology</a> and the father of <a href="/wiki/Thomism" title="Thomism">Thomism</a>; of which he argued that <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a> is found in God. His influence on <a href="/wiki/Western_thought" class="mw-redirect" title="Western thought">Western thought</a> is considerable, and much of <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">modern philosophy</a> developed or opposed his ideas, particularly in the areas of ethics, <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, and political theory.
</p><p>Unlike many currents in the Church of the time,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Thomas embraced several ideas put forward by <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>‚Äîwhom he called "the Philosopher"‚Äîand attempted to synthesize <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian philosophy</a> with the principles of Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His best-known works are the <i>Disputed Questions on Truth</i> (1256‚Äì1259), the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_contra_Gentiles" title="Summa contra Gentiles">Summa contra Gentiles</a></i> (1259‚Äì1265), and the unfinished but massively influential <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa Theologica</a></i> aka <i>Summa Theologiae</i> (1265‚Äì1274). His commentaries on <a href="/wiki/Scripture" class="mw-redirect" title="Scripture">Scripture</a> and on Aristotle also form an important part of his body of work. Furthermore, Thomas is distinguished for his <a href="/wiki/Eucharistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Eucharistic">eucharistic</a> hymns, which form a part of the Church's liturgy.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> honors Thomas Aquinas as a <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saint</a> and regards him as the model teacher for those studying for the priesthood, and indeed the highest expression of both natural reason and <a href="/wiki/Speculative_reason" title="Speculative reason">speculative</a> <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>. In modern times, under papal directives, the study of his works was long used as a core of the required program of study for those seeking ordination as priests or deacons, as well as for those in religious formation and for other students of the sacred disciplines (philosophy, Catholic theology, church history, liturgy, and <a href="/wiki/Canon_law_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Canon law (Catholic Church)">canon law</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas Aquinas is considered one of the Catholic Church's greatest theologians and philosophers. <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XV" title="Pope Benedict XV">Pope Benedict XV</a> declared: "This (Dominican) Order<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... acquired new luster when the Church declared the teaching of Thomas to be her own and that Doctor, honored with the special praises of the Pontiffs, the master and patron of Catholic schools."<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The English philosopher <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Kenny" title="Anthony Kenny">Anthony Kenny</a> considers Thomas to be "one of the dozen greatest philosophers of the western world".<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Early_life_.281225.E2.80.931244.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_(1225‚Äì1244)">Early life (1225‚Äì1244)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life (1225‚Äì1244)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Thomas was most probably not born in the castle of <a href="/wiki/Roccasecca" title="Roccasecca">Roccasecca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aquino,_Italy" title="Aquino, Italy">Aquino</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">Kingdom of Sicily</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Lazio" title="Lazio">Lazio</a>, Italy), <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1225</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> According to some authors,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (November 2015)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> he was born in the castle of his father, Landulf of Aquino. He was born to the most powerful branch of the family, Landulf of Aquino was a man of means. As a knight in the service of <a href="/wiki/Roger_II_of_Sicily" title="Roger II of Sicily">King Roger II</a>, he held the title <i><a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">miles</a></i>. Thomas's mother, Theodora, belonged to the Rossi branch of the <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Neapolitan</a> Caracciolo family.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Landulf's brother Sinibald was <a href="/wiki/Abbot" title="Abbot">abbot</a> of the first <a href="/wiki/Order_of_St._Benedictine" class="mw-redirect" title="Order of St. Benedictine">Benedictine</a> <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monastery</a> at <a href="/wiki/Monte_Cassino" title="Monte Cassino">Monte Cassino</a>. While the rest of the family's sons pursued military careers,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> the family intended for Thomas to follow his uncle into the abbacy;<sup id="cite_ref-stump3_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stump3-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> this would have been a normal career path for a younger son of southern Italian nobility.<sup id="cite_ref-schaff-422_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schaff-422-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the age of five Thomas began his early education at Monte Cassino but after the military conflict between the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Frederick_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Emperor Frederick II">Emperor Frederick II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_IX" title="Pope Gregory IX">Pope Gregory IX</a> spilled into the abbey in early 1239, Landulf and Theodora had Thomas enrolled at the <i><a href="/wiki/Studium_generale" title="Studium generale">studium generale</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/University_of_Naples_Federico_II" title="University of Naples Federico II">university</a>) recently established by Frederick in <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> It was here that Thomas was probably introduced to <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a>, all of whom would influence his theological philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-davies2_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> It was also during his study at Naples that Thomas came under the influence of John of St. Julian, a Dominican preacher in Naples, who was part of the active effort by the Dominican order to recruit devout followers.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> There his teacher in arithmetic, geometry, astronomy, and music was <a href="/wiki/Petrus_de_Ibernia" title="Petrus de Ibernia">Petrus de Ibernia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG/220px-Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG/330px-Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG/440px-Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG 2x" data-file-width="852" data-file-height="639" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Castello_di_Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano_9.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Castle of <a href="/wiki/Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano" title="Monte San Giovanni Campano">Monte San Giovanni Campano</a></div></div></div>
<p>At the age of nineteen Thomas resolved to join the recently founded <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican Order</a>. Thomas's change of heart did not please his family.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In an attempt to prevent Theodora's interference in Thomas's choice, the Dominicans arranged to move Thomas to Rome, and from Rome, to Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-Hampden23_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hampden23-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> However, while on his journey to Rome, per Theodora's instructions, his brothers seized him as he was drinking from a spring and took him back to his parents at the castle of <a href="/wiki/Monte_San_Giovanni_Campano" title="Monte San Giovanni Campano">Monte San Giovanni Campano</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hampden23_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hampden23-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas was held prisoner for almost one year in the family castles at Monte San Giovanni and Roccasecca in an attempt to prevent him from assuming the Dominican habit and to push him into renouncing his new aspiration.<sup id="cite_ref-davies2_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Political concerns prevented the Pope from ordering Thomas's release, which had the effect of extending Thomas's detention.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Thomas passed this time of trial tutoring his sisters and communicating with members of the Dominican Order.<sup id="cite_ref-davies2_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies2-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Family members became desperate to dissuade Thomas, who remained determined to join the Dominicans. At one point, two of his brothers resorted to the measure of hiring a prostitute to seduce him. According to legend, Thomas drove her away wielding a fire iron and two angels appeared to him as he slept and strengthened his determination to remain celibate.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg/220px-Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg/330px-Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg/440px-Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="956" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Thomas_Aquinas_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez" title="Diego Vel√°zquez">Diego Vel√°zquez</a>, Thomas is girded by angels with a mystical belt of purity after his <a href="/wiki/Chastity" title="Chastity">proof of chastity</a></div></div></div>
<p>By 1244, seeing that all her attempts to dissuade Thomas had failed, Theodora sought to save the family's dignity, arranging for Thomas to escape at night through his window. In her mind, a secret escape from detention was less damaging than an open surrender to the Dominicans. Thomas was sent first to Naples and then to Rome to meet <a href="/wiki/Johannes_von_Wildeshausen" class="mw-redirect" title="Johannes von Wildeshausen">Johannes von Wildeshausen</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Master_General_of_the_Dominican_Order" class="mw-redirect" title="Master General of the Dominican Order">Master General of the Dominican Order</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Paris.2C_Cologne.2C_Albert_Magnus.2C_and_first_Paris_regency_.281245.E2.80.931259.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Paris,_Cologne,_Albert_Magnus,_and_first_Paris_regency_(1245‚Äì1259)">Paris, Cologne, Albert Magnus, and first Paris regency (1245‚Äì1259)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Paris, Cologne, Albert Magnus, and first Paris regency (1245‚Äì1259)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1245 Thomas was sent to study at the Faculty of the Arts at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a>, where he most likely met Dominican scholar <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-healy2_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-healy2-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> then the holder of the Chair of Theology at the College of St. James in Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> When Albertus was sent by his superiors to teach at the new <i>studium generale</i> at Cologne in 1248,<sup id="cite_ref-healy2_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-healy2-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Thomas followed him, declining <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_IV" title="Pope Innocent IV">Pope Innocent IV</a>'s offer to appoint him abbot of Monte Cassino as a Dominican.<sup id="cite_ref-stump3_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stump3-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Albertus then appointed the reluctant Thomas <i>magister studentium</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-schaff-422_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schaff-422-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Because Thomas was quiet and didn't speak much, some of his fellow students thought he was slow. But Albertus prophetically exclaimed: "You call him the dumb ox, but in his teaching he will one day produce such a bellowing that it will be heard throughout the world."<sup id="cite_ref-stump3_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stump3-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas taught in Cologne as an apprentice professor (<i>baccalaureus biblicus</i>), instructing students on the books of the Old Testament and writing <i>Expositio super Isaiam ad litteram</i> (<i>Literal Commentary on Isaiah</i>), <i>Postilla super Ieremiam</i> (<i>Commentary on Jeremiah</i>) and <i>Postilla super Threnos</i> (<i>Commentary on Lamentations</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Then in 1252 he returned to Paris to study for the master's degree in theology. He lectured on the Bible as an apprentice professor, and upon becoming a <i>baccalaureus Sententiarum</i> (bachelor of the <i>Sentences</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> he devoted his final three years of study to commenting on <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lombard" title="Peter Lombard">Peter Lombard</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Sentences" title="Sentences">Sentences</a></i>. In the first of his four theological syntheses, Thomas composed a massive commentary on the <i>Sentences</i> titled <i>Scriptum super libros Sententiarium</i> (<i>Commentary on the Sentences</i>). Aside from his masters writings, he wrote <i>De ente et essentia</i> (<i>On Being and Essence</i>) for his fellow Dominicans in Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-stump3_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stump3-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the spring of 1256 Thomas was appointed regent master in theology at Paris and one of his first works upon assuming this office was <i>Contra impugnantes Dei cultum et religionem</i> (<i>Against Those Who Assail the Worship of God and Religion</i>), defending the <a href="/wiki/Mendicant_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Mendicant order">mendicant orders</a>, which had come under attack by <a href="/wiki/William_of_Saint-Amour" title="William of Saint-Amour">William of Saint-Amour</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> During his tenure from 1256 to 1259, Thomas wrote numerous works, including: <i>Questiones disputatae de veritate</i> (<i>Disputed Questions on Truth</i>), a collection of twenty-nine disputed questions on aspects of faith and the human condition<sup id="cite_ref-stump4_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stump4-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> prepared for the public university debates he presided over on <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">Lent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Advent" title="Advent">Advent</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> <i>Quaestiones quodlibetales</i> (<i>Quodlibetal Questions</i>), a collection of his responses to questions posed to him by the academic audience;<sup id="cite_ref-stump4_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stump4-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> and both <i>Expositio super librum Boethii De trinitate</i> (<i>Commentary on Boethius's De trinitate</i>) and <i>Expositio super librum Boethii De hebdomadibus</i> (<i>Commentary on Boethius's De hebdomadibus</i>), commentaries on the works of 6th-century Roman philosopher <a href="/wiki/Anicius_Manlius_Severinus_Boethius" class="mw-redirect" title="Anicius Manlius Severinus Boethius">Boethius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-stumpxvii_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stumpxvii-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> By the end of his regency, Thomas was working on one of his most famous works, <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_contra_Gentiles" title="Summa contra Gentiles">Summa contra Gentiles</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-davies4_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies4-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Naples.2C_Orvieto.2C_Rome_.281259.E2.80.931268.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Naples,_Orvieto,_Rome_(1259‚Äì1268)">Naples, Orvieto, Rome (1259‚Äì1268)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Naples, Orvieto, Rome (1259‚Äì1268)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1259 Thomas completed his first regency at the <i>studium generale</i> and left Paris so that others in his order could gain this teaching experience. He returned to Naples where he was appointed as general preacher by the provincial chapter of 29 September 1260. In September 1261 he was called to <a href="/wiki/Orvieto" title="Orvieto">Orvieto</a> as conventual lector he was responsible for the pastoral formation of the friars unable to attend a <i><a href="/wiki/Studium_generale" title="Studium generale">studium generale</a></i>. In Orvieto Thomas completed his <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_contra_Gentiles" title="Summa contra Gentiles">Summa contra Gentiles</a></i>, wrote the <i>Catena aurea</i> (<i>The Golden Chain</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-healy4_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-healy4-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and produced works for <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_IV" title="Pope Urban IV">Pope Urban IV</a> such as the liturgy for the newly created <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_Corpus_Christi" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast of Corpus Christi">feast of Corpus Christi</a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Contra_Errores_Graecorum" title="Contra Errores Graecorum">Contra errores graecorum</a></i> (<i>Against the Errors of the Greeks</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-davies4_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies4-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Some of the hymns that Thomas wrote for the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_Corpus_Christi" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast of Corpus Christi">feast of Corpus Christi</a> are still sung today, such as the <i>Pange lingua</i> (whose penultimate verse is the famous <i>Tantum ergo</i>), and <i>Panis angelicus</i>. Modern scholarship has confirmed that Thomas was indeed the author of these texts, a point that some had contested.<sup id="cite_ref-Torrell_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Torrell-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 1265 the newly elected <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_IV" title="Pope Clement IV">Pope Clement IV</a> summoned Thomas to Rome to serve as papal theologian. This same year he was ordered by the Dominican Chapter of Agnani<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> to teach at the <i><a href="/wiki/Medieval_university" title="Medieval university">studium</a></i> <i>conventuale</i> at the Roman <a href="/wiki/Convent" title="Convent">convent</a> of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a>, founded some years before, in 1222.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The <i>studium</i> at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> now became an experiment for the Dominicans, the Order's first <i>studium provinciale</i>, an intermediate school between the <i>studium conventuale</i> and the <i>studium generale</i>. Prior to this time the Roman Province had offered no specialized education of any sort, no arts, no philosophy; only simple convent schools, with their basic courses in theology for resident friars, were functioning in Tuscany and the meridionale during the first several decades of the order's life. The new <i>studium provinciale</i> at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> was to be a more advanced school for the province.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bartholomew_of_Lucca" title="Bartholomew of Lucca">Tolomeo da Lucca</a>, an associate and early biographer of Thomas, tells us that at the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> <i>studium</i> Thomas taught the full range of philosophical subjects, both moral and natural.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While at the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> <i>studium provinciale</i> Thomas began his most famous work, the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa theologiae</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-healy4_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-healy4-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> which he conceived specifically suited to beginning students: "Because a doctor of Catholic truth ought not only to teach the proficient, but to him pertains also to instruct beginners. As the Apostle says in 1 Corinthians 3:1‚Äì2, <i>as to infants in Christ, I gave you milk to drink, not meat</i>, our proposed intention in this work is to convey those things that pertain to the Christian religion in a way that is fitting to the instruction of beginners."<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> While there he also wrote a variety of other works like his unfinished <i>Compendium Theologiae</i> and <i>Responsio ad fr. Ioannem Vercellensem de articulis 108 sumptis ex opere Petri de Tarentasia</i> (<i>Reply to Brother John of Vercelli Regarding 108 Articles Drawn from the Work of Peter of Tarentaise</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-stumpxvii_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stumpxvii-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In his position as head of the <i>studium</i> Thomas conducted a series of important disputations on the power of God, which he compiled into his <i>De potentia</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-davies5_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies5-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Nicholas Brunacci [1240‚Äì1322] was among Thomas's students at the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> <i>studium provinciale</i> and later at the Paris <i>studium generale</i>. In November 1268 he was with Thomas and his associate and secretary <a href="/wiki/Reginald_of_Piperno" title="Reginald of Piperno">Reginald of Piperno</a>, as they left Viterbo on their way to Paris to begin the academic year.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Another student of Thomas's at the Santa Sabina <i>studium provinciale</i> was <a href="/wiki/Beatification" title="Beatification">Blessed</a> Tommasello da Perugia.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas remained at the <i>studium</i> at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> from 1265 until he was called back to Paris in 1268 for a second teaching regency.<sup id="cite_ref-davies5_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies5-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> With his departure for Paris in 1268 and the passage of time the pedagogical activities of the <i>studium provinciale</i> at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> were divided between two campuses. A new <a href="/wiki/Convent" title="Convent">convent</a> of the Order at the Church of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_sopra_Minerva" title="Santa Maria sopra Minerva">Santa Maria sopra Minerva</a> had a modest beginning in 1255 as a community for women converts, but grew rapidly in size and importance after being given over to the Dominicans friars in 1275.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In 1288 the theology component of the provincial curriculum for the education of the friars was relocated from the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> <i>studium provinciale</i> to the <i>studium conventuale</i> at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_sopra_Minerva" title="Santa Maria sopra Minerva">Santa Maria sopra Minerva</a>, which was redesignated as a <i>studium particularis theologiae</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> This <i>studium</i> was transformed in the 16th century into the College of Saint Thomas (<a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Collegium Divi Thom√¶</i>). In the 20th century the college was relocated to the convent of <a href="/wiki/Santi_Domenico_e_Sisto" title="Santi Domenico e Sisto">Saints Dominic and Sixtus</a> and was transformed into the <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_University_of_Saint_Thomas_Aquinas" title="Pontifical University of Saint Thomas Aquinas">Pontifical University of Saint Thomas Aquinas, <i>Angelicum</i></a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Quarrelsome_second_Paris_regency_.281269.E2.80.931272.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Quarrelsome_second_Paris_regency_(1269‚Äì1272)">Quarrelsome second Paris regency (1269‚Äì1272)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Quarrelsome second Paris regency (1269‚Äì1272)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg/150px-Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="340" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg/225px-Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg/300px-Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg 2x" data-file-width="570" data-file-height="1293" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Benozzo_Gozzoli_-_Triumph_of_St_Thomas_Aquinas_-_WGA10334.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Triumph of St Thomas Aquinas</i>, "Doctor Communis", between Plato and Aristotle, <a href="/wiki/Benozzo_Gozzoli" title="Benozzo Gozzoli">Benozzo Gozzoli</a>,1471. Louvre, Paris</div></div></div>
<p>In 1268 the Dominican order assigned Thomas to be regent master at the University of Paris for a second time, a position he held until the spring of 1272. Part of the reason for this sudden reassignment appears to have arisen from the rise of "<a href="/wiki/Averroism" title="Averroism">Averroism</a>" or "radical <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a>" in the universities. In response to these perceived errors, Thomas wrote two works, one of them being <i>De unitate intellectus, contra Averroistas</i> (<i>On the Unity of Intellect, against the Averroists</i>) in which he reprimands Averroism as incompatible with Christian doctrine.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> During his second regency, he finished the second part of the <i>Summa</i> and wrote <i>De virtutibus</i> and <i>De aeternitate mundi, contra murmurantes</i> (<i>On the Eternity of the World, against Grumblers</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-davies5_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies5-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> the latter of which dealt with controversial Averroist and Aristotelian <i>beginninglessness</i> of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-stump11_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stump11-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Disputes with some important <a href="/wiki/Franciscans" title="Franciscans">Franciscans</a> conspired to make his second regency much more difficult and troubled than the first. A year before Thomas re-assumed the regency at the 1266‚Äì67 Paris disputations, Franciscan master William of Baglione accused Thomas of encouraging Averroists, most likely counting him as one of the "blind leaders of the blind". <a href="/wiki/Eleonore_Stump" title="Eleonore Stump">Eleonore Stump</a> says, "It has also been persuasively argued that Aquinas's <i>De aeternitate mundi</i> was directed in particular against his Franciscan colleague in theology, <a href="/wiki/John_Pecham" class="mw-redirect" title="John Pecham">John Pecham</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-stump11_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stump11-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In reality, Thomas was deeply disturbed by the spread of Averroism and was angered when he discovered <a href="/wiki/Siger_of_Brabant" title="Siger of Brabant">Siger of Brabant</a> teaching Averroistic interpretations of Aristotle to Parisian students.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
On 10 December 1270, the Bishop of Paris, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Tempier" title="√âtienne Tempier">√âtienne Tempier</a>, issued an edict condemning thirteen Aristotelian and Averroistic propositions as heretical and excommunicating anyone who continued to support them.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Many in the ecclesiastical community, the so-called Augustinians, were fearful that this introduction of Aristotelianism and the more extreme Averroism might somehow contaminate the purity of the Christian faith. In what appears to be an attempt to counteract the growing fear of Aristotelian thought, Thomas conducted a series of disputations between 1270 and 1272: <i>De virtutibus in communi</i> (<i>On Virtues in General</i>), <i>De virtutibus cardinalibus</i> (<i>On Cardinal Virtues</i>), <i>De spe</i> (<i>On Hope</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Final_days_and_.22straw.22_.281272.E2.80.931274.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_days_and_&quot;straw&quot;_(1272‚Äì1274)">Final days and "straw" (1272‚Äì1274)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Final days and &quot;straw&quot; (1272‚Äì1274)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="box-Fanpov plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-fanpov" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>may be written from a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Fancruft" title="Wikipedia:Fancruft">fan's point of view</a>, rather than a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutral point of view</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Cleanup" title="Wikipedia:Cleanup">clean it up</a> to conform to a <a href="/wiki/Category:Wikipedia_style_guidelines" title="Category:Wikipedia style guidelines">higher standard</a> of quality, and to make it neutral in tone.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2019</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Patrick_Church_(Columbus,_Ohio)_-_stained_glass,_St._Thomas_Aquinas,_detail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Saint_Patrick_Church_%28Columbus%2C_Ohio%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg/200px-Saint_Patrick_Church_%28Columbus%2C_Ohio%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="356" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Saint_Patrick_Church_%28Columbus%2C_Ohio%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg/300px-Saint_Patrick_Church_%28Columbus%2C_Ohio%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Saint_Patrick_Church_%28Columbus%2C_Ohio%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg/400px-Saint_Patrick_Church_%28Columbus%2C_Ohio%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2017" data-file-height="3587" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Patrick_Church_(Columbus,_Ohio)_-_stained_glass,_St._Thomas_Aquinas,_detail.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Icon of the crucifixion speaking to Thomas Aquinas is depicted on this stained glass window in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick_Church_(Columbus,_Ohio)" title="Saint Patrick Church (Columbus, Ohio)">Saint Patrick Church (Columbus, Ohio)</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1272 Thomas took leave from the University of Paris when the Dominicans from his home province called upon him to establish a <i>studium generale</i> wherever he liked and staff it as he pleased. He chose to establish the institution in Naples, and moved there to take his post as regent master.<sup id="cite_ref-davies5_49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davies5-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> He took his time at Naples to work on the third part of the <i>Summa</i> while giving lectures on various religious topics. He also preached to the people of Naples every day in Lent, 1273. These sermons on the Commandments, the Creed, the Our Father, and Hail Mary were very popular.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On one occasion, in 1273 at the Dominican convent of Naples in the chapel of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Nicholas" title="Saint Nicholas">Saint Nicholas</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> after <a href="/wiki/Matins" title="Matins">Matins</a>, Thomas lingered and was seen by the <a href="/wiki/Sacristan" title="Sacristan">sacristan</a> Domenic of Caserta to be levitating in prayer with tears before an icon of the crucified Christ. Christ said to Thomas, "You have written well of me, Thomas. What reward would you have for your labor?" Thomas responded, "Nothing but you, Lord."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> After this exchange something happened, but Thomas never spoke of it or wrote it down.
</p><p>On 6 December 1273, another mystical experience took place. While he was celebrating Mass, he experienced an unusually long ecstasy.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Because of what he saw, he abandoned his routine and refused to dictate to his <i>socius</i> <a href="/wiki/Reginald_of_Piperno" title="Reginald of Piperno">Reginald of Piperno</a>. When Reginald begged him to get back to work, Thomas replied: "Reginald, I cannot, because all that I have written seems like straw to me"<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> (<i>mihi videtur ut palea</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> As a result, the Summa Theologica would remain uncompleted. What exactly triggered Thomas's change in behavior is believed by Catholics to have been some kind of supernatural experience of God.<sup id="cite_ref-Stanford_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> After taking to his bed, he did recover some strength.<sup id="cite_ref--healy7_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note--healy7-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1054 the <a href="/wiki/East-West_Schism" class="mw-redirect" title="East-West Schism">Great Schism</a> had occurred between the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Church</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a> (known as the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>) in the West, and the <a href="/wiki/Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarchate of Constantinople">Patriarchate of Constantinople</a> in the East (known as the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a>). Looking to find a way to reunite the Eastern Orthodox Church and the Roman Catholic Church, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_X" title="Pope Gregory X">Pope Gregory X</a> convened the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Lyon" title="Second Council of Lyon">Second Council of Lyon</a> to be held on 1 May 1274 and summoned Thomas to attend.<sup id="cite_ref-nichols18_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nichols18-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> At the meeting, Thomas's work for Pope Urban IV concerning the Greeks, <i>Contra errores graecorum</i>, was to be presented.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On his way to the Council, riding on a donkey along the <a href="/wiki/Appian_Way" title="Appian Way">Appian Way</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-nichols18_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nichols18-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> he struck his head on the branch of a fallen tree and became seriously ill again. He was then quickly escorted to <a href="/wiki/Monte_Cassino" title="Monte Cassino">Monte Cassino</a> to convalesce.<sup id="cite_ref--healy7_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note--healy7-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> After resting for a while, he set out again, but stopped at the <a href="/wiki/Cistercian" class="mw-redirect" title="Cistercian">Cistercian</a> <a href="/wiki/Fossanova_Abbey" title="Fossanova Abbey">Fossanova Abbey</a> after again falling ill.<sup id="cite_ref-healy8_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-healy8-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The monks nursed him for several days,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> and as he received his <a href="/wiki/Last_rites" title="Last rites">last rites</a> he prayed: "I have written and taught much about this very holy Body, and about the other sacraments in the faith of Christ, and about the Holy Roman Church, to whose correction I expose and submit everything I have written."<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> He died on 7 March 1274<sup id="cite_ref-healy8_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-healy8-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> while giving commentary on the <a href="/wiki/Song_of_Songs" title="Song of Songs">Song of Songs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_of_levitation">Claims of levitation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Claims of levitation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Saints_and_levitation" title="Saints and levitation">Saints and levitation</a></div>
<p>For centuries, there have been recurring claims that Thomas had the ability to <a href="/wiki/Levitation_(paranormal)" title="Levitation (paranormal)">levitate</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/G.K._Chesterton" class="mw-redirect" title="G.K. Chesterton">G.K. Chesterton</a> wrote that, "His experiences included well-attested cases of levitation in ecstasy; and the Blessed Virgin appeared to him, comforting him with the welcome news that he would never be a Bishop."<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Condemnation_of_1277">Condemnation of 1277</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Condemnation of 1277">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Condemnations_of_1210%E2%80%931277" title="Condemnations of 1210‚Äì1277">Condemnations of 1210‚Äì1277</a></div>
<p>In 1277 <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Tempier" title="√âtienne Tempier">√âtienne Tempier</a>, the same bishop of Paris who had issued the condemnation of 1270, issued another more extensive condemnation. One aim of this condemnation was to clarify that God's absolute power transcended any principles of logic that <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> or <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a> might place on it.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> More specifically, it contained a list of 219 propositions that the bishop had determined to violate the omnipotence of God, and included in this list were twenty Thomistic propositions. Their inclusion badly damaged Thomas's reputation for many years.<sup id="cite_ref-Kung112_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kung112-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divine Comedy</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante</a> sees the glorified soul of Thomas in the Heaven of the Sun with the other great exemplars of religious wisdom.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dante" class="mw-redirect" title="Dante">Dante</a> asserts that Thomas died by poisoning, on the order of <a href="/wiki/Charles_of_Anjou" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles of Anjou">Charles of Anjou</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Villani (ix. 218) cites this belief, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Anonimo_Fiorentino" class="mw-redirect" title="Anonimo Fiorentino">Anonimo Fiorentino</a></i> describes the crime and its motive. But the historian <a href="/wiki/Ludovico_Antonio_Muratori" title="Ludovico Antonio Muratori">Ludovico Antonio Muratori</a> reproduces the account made by one of Thomas's friends, and this version of the story gives no hint of foul play.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica-250_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-250-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas's theology had begun its rise to prestige. Two centuries later, in 1567, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_V" title="Pope Pius V">Pope Pius V</a> proclaimed St. Thomas Aquinas a <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_the_Church" title="Doctor of the Church">Doctor of the Church</a> and ranked his feast with those of the four great Latin fathers: <a href="/wiki/Ambrose" title="Ambrose">Ambrose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_I" title="Pope Gregory I">Gregory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica-250_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-250-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> At the Council of Trent, Thomas had the honor of having his <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa theologiae</a></i> placed on the altar alongside the Bible and the <a href="/wiki/Decretals" class="mw-redirect" title="Decretals">Decretals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kung112_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kung112-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mullady_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mullady-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his <a href="/wiki/Encyclical" title="Encyclical">encyclical</a> of 4 August 1879, <i><a href="/wiki/Aeterni_Patris" title="Aeterni Patris">Aeterni Patris</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_XIII" title="Pope Leo XIII">Pope Leo XIII</a> stated that Thomas Aquinas's theology was a definitive exposition of Catholic doctrine. Thus, he directed the clergy to take the teachings of Thomas as the basis of their theological positions. Leo XIII also decreed that all Catholic seminaries and universities must teach Thomas's doctrines, and where Thomas did not speak on a topic, the teachers were "urged to teach conclusions that were reconcilable with his thinking." In 1880, Saint Thomas Aquinas was declared patron of all Catholic educational establishments.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica-250_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-250-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canonization">Canonization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Canonization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg/220px-Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg/330px-Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg/440px-Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1192" data-file-height="1251" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrea_di_Bonaiuto._Santa_Maria_Novella_1366-7_fresco_0001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Triumph of St. Thomas Aquinas</i>, "Doctor Angelicus", with saints and angels, <a href="/wiki/Andrea_di_Bonaiuto" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrea di Bonaiuto">Andrea di Bonaiuto</a>, 1366. <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Santa_Maria_Novella" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilica of Santa Maria Novella">Basilica of Santa Maria Novella</a>, fresco</div></div></div>
<p>When the <a href="/wiki/Devil%27s_advocate" title="Devil&#39;s advocate">devil's advocate</a> at his <a href="/wiki/Canonization" title="Canonization">canonization</a> process objected that there were no <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">miracles</a>, one of the cardinals answered, "<i>Tot miraculis, quot articulis</i>"‚Äî"there are as many miracles (in his life) as articles (in his <a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica"><i>Summa</i></a>)".<sup id="cite_ref-Mullady_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mullady-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Fifty years after Thomas's death, on 18 July 1323, <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XXII" title="Pope John XXII">Pope John XXII</a>, seated in <a href="/wiki/Avignon" title="Avignon">Avignon</a>, pronounced Thomas a <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saint</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A monastery at Naples, near the cathedral of <a href="/wiki/St._Januarius" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Januarius">St. Januarius</a>, shows a cell in which he supposedly lived.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica-250_78-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-250-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> His remains were placed in the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Jacobins" title="Church of the Jacobins">Church of the Jacobins</a> in <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">Toulouse</a> on 28 January 1369. Between 1789 and 1974, they were held in the <a href="/wiki/Basilique_de_Saint-Sernin,_Toulouse" class="mw-redirect" title="Basilique de Saint-Sernin, Toulouse">Basilique de Saint-Sernin, Toulouse</a>. In 1974, they were returned to the Church of the Jacobins, where they have remained ever since.
</p><p>When he was canonized, his feast day was inserted in the <a href="/wiki/General_Roman_Calendar" title="General Roman Calendar">General Roman Calendar</a> for celebration on 7 March, the day of his death. Since this date commonly falls within <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">Lent</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mysterii_Paschalis" title="Mysterii Paschalis">1969 revision of the calendar</a> moved his <a href="/wiki/Memorial_(liturgy)" title="Memorial (liturgy)">memorial</a> to 28 January, the date of the translation of his relics to <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Jacobins" title="Church of the Jacobins">Church of the Jacobins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">Toulouse</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas Aquinas is honored with a <a href="/wiki/Feast_day" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast day">feast day</a> in some churches of the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks plainlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><td style="padding:0.4em 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Emblem_of_the_Papacy_SE.svg" class="image"><img alt="Emblem of the Papacy SE.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Emblem_of_the_Papacy_SE.svg/25px-Emblem_of_the_Papacy_SE.svg.png" decoding="async" width="25" height="34" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Emblem_of_the_Papacy_SE.svg/38px-Emblem_of_the_Papacy_SE.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Emblem_of_the_Papacy_SE.svg/50px-Emblem_of_the_Papacy_SE.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="815" data-file-height="1105" /></a></td></tr><tr><td style="padding-top:0.2em;line-height:1.2em">Part of a <a href="/wiki/Category:Thomas_Aquinas" title="Category:Thomas Aquinas">series</a> on</td></tr><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;padding-top:0;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em"><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Thomas Aquinas</a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;display:block;margin-bottom:0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benozzo_Gozzoli_004a.jpg" class="image" title="Detail from &quot;Triumph of St. Thomas Aquinas over Averroes&quot; </tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;border:none;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/File:046CupolaSPietro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="046CupolaSPietro.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/16px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg" decoding="async" width="16" height="12" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/24px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/32px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Portal:Catholicism">Catholicism&#32;portal</a></li>

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thomism" title="Thomism">Thomism</a></div>
<p>Thomas Aquinas was a theologian and a <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">Scholastic</a> philosopher.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> However, he never considered himself a philosopher, and criticized philosophers, whom he saw as pagans, for always "falling short of the true and proper wisdom to be found in Christian revelation."<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> With this in mind, Thomas did have respect for Aristotle, so much so that in the <i>Summa</i>, he often cites Aristotle simply as "the Philosopher", a designation frequently used at that time. Much of his work bears upon philosophical topics, and in this sense may be characterized as philosophical. Thomas's philosophical thought has exerted enormous influence on subsequent Christian theology, especially that of the Catholic Church, extending to Western philosophy in general. Thomas stands as a vehicle and modifier of <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a>. It is said that Thomas modified both <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a> by way of heavy reliance on the <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Dionysius" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudo-Dionysius">Pseudo-Dionysius</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commentaries_on_Aristotle">Commentaries on Aristotle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Commentaries on Aristotle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Thomas Aquinas wrote several important commentaries on <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s works, including <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Soul" title="On the Soul">On the Soul</a></i>, <i>On Interpretation</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Metaphysics_(Aristotle)" title="Metaphysics (Aristotle)">Metaphysics</a></i>. His work is associated with <a href="/wiki/William_of_Moerbeke" title="William of Moerbeke">William of Moerbeke</a>'s translations of Aristotle from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> into <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemology">Epistemology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Epistemology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Double_truth" title="Double truth">Double truth</a></div>
<p>Thomas Aquinas believed "that for the knowledge of any truth whatsoever man needs divine help, that the intellect may be moved by God to its act."<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> However, he believed that human beings have the natural capacity to know many things without special <a href="/wiki/Divine_revelation" class="mw-redirect" title="Divine revelation">divine revelation</a>, even though such revelation occurs from time to time, "especially in regard to such (truths) as pertain to faith."<sup id="cite_ref-Summa_I-II_Q109a1_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summa_I-II_Q109a1-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> But this is the light that is given to man by God according to man's nature: "Now every form bestowed on created things by God has power for a determined act[uality], which it can bring about in proportion to its own proper endowment; and beyond which it is powerless, except by a superadded form, as water can only heat when heated by the fire. And thus the human understanding has a form, viz. intelligible light, which of itself is sufficient for knowing certain intelligible things, viz. those we can come to know through the senses."<sup id="cite_ref-Summa_I-II_Q109a1_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summa_I-II_Q109a1-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics">Ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christian_ethics" title="Christian ethics">Christian ethics</a></div>
<p>Thomas's ethics are based on the concept of "first principles of action".<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In his <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa theologiae</a></i>, he wrote:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Virtue denotes a certain perfection of a power. Now a thing's perfection is considered chiefly in regard to its end. But the end of power is act. Wherefore power is said to be perfect, according as it is determinate to its act.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Thomas emphasized that "<a href="/wiki/Synderesis" title="Synderesis">Synderesis</a> is said to be the law of our mind, because it is a habit containing the precepts of the natural law, which are the first principles of human actions."<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Thomas "...<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>all acts of virtue are prescribed by the natural law: since each one's reason naturally dictates to him to act virtuously. But if we speak of virtuous acts, considered in themselves, i.e., in their proper species, thus not all virtuous acts are prescribed by the natural law: for many things are done virtuously, to which nature does not incline at first; but that, through the inquiry of reason, have been found by men to be conductive to well living." Therefore, we must determine if we are speaking of virtuous acts as under the aspect of virtuous or as an act in its species.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas defined the four <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_virtues" title="Cardinal virtues">cardinal virtues</a> as <a href="/wiki/Prudence" title="Prudence">prudence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Temperance_(virtue)" title="Temperance (virtue)">temperance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">justice</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Courage" title="Courage">fortitude</a>. The cardinal virtues are natural and revealed in nature, and they are binding on everyone. There are, however, three <a href="/wiki/Theological_virtues" title="Theological virtues">theological virtues</a>: <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hope" title="Hope">hope</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Charity_(virtue)" title="Charity (virtue)">charity</a>. Thomas also describes the virtues as imperfect (incomplete) and perfect (complete) virtues. A perfect virtue is any virtue with charity, charity completes a cardinal virtue. A non-Christian can display courage, but it would be courage with temperance. A Christian would display courage with charity. These are somewhat supernatural and are distinct from other virtues in their object, namely, God:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Now the object of the theological virtues is God Himself, Who is the last end of all, as surpassing the knowledge of our reason. On the other hand, the object of the intellectual and moral virtues is something comprehensible to human reason. Wherefore the theological virtues are specifically distinct from the moral and intellectual virtues.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Thomas Aquinas wrote "[Greed] is a sin against God, just as all mortal sins, in as much as man condemns things eternal for the sake of temporal things."<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Treatise_on_Law" title="Treatise on Law">Treatise on Law</a></i>, Thomas distinguished four kinds of law: eternal, <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural</a>, human, and <a href="/wiki/Divine_law" title="Divine law">divine</a>. <i>Eternal law</i> is the decree of God that governs all creation. It is, "That Law which is the Supreme Reason cannot be understood to be otherwise than unchangeable and eternal."<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> <i>Natural law</i> is the human "participation" in the <i>eternal law</i> and is discovered by <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> <i>Natural law</i> is based on "<a href="/wiki/First_principles" class="mw-redirect" title="First principles">first principles</a>":
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>. . . this is the first precept of the law, that good is to be done and promoted, and evil is to be avoided. All other precepts of the natural law are based on this . . .<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Whether the natural law contains several precepts, or one only is explained by Thomas, "All the inclinations of any parts whatsoever of human nature, e.g., of the concupiscible and irascible parts, in so far as they are ruled by reason, belong to the natural law, and are reduced to one first precept, as stated above: so that the precepts of the natural law are many in themselves, but are based on one common foundation."<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The desires to live and to procreate are counted by Thomas among those basic (natural) human values on which all human values are based. According to Thomas, all human tendencies are geared towards real human goods. In this case, the human nature in question is marriage, the total gift of oneself to another that ensures a family for children and a future for mankind.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> He defined the dual inclination of the action of love: "towards the good which a man wishes to someone (to himself or to another) and towards that to which he wishes some good".<sup id="cite_ref-newadvent.org_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newadvent.org-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Concerning the Human Law, Thomas concludes, "...<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>that just as, in the speculative reason, from naturally known indemonstrable principles, we draw the conclusions of the various sciences, the knowledge of which is not imparted to us by nature, but acquired by the efforts of reason, so to it is from the precepts of the natural law, as from general and indemonstrable principles, that human reason needs to proceed to the more particular determination of certain matters. These particular determinations, devised by human reason, are called human laws, provided the other essential conditions of law be observed<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>..." Human law is <a href="/wiki/Positive_law" title="Positive law">positive law</a>: the natural law applied by governments to societies.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural and human law is not adequate alone. The need for human behavior to be directed made it necessary to have Divine law. Divine law is the specially revealed law in the <a href="/wiki/Scriptures" class="mw-redirect" title="Scriptures">scriptures</a>. Thomas quotes, "The Apostle says (Hebrews 7.12): The priesthood being translated, it is necessary that a translation also be made of the law. But the priesthood is twofold, as stated in the same passage, viz, the levitical priesthood, and the priesthood of Christ. Therefore the Divine law is twofold, namely, the Old Law and the New Law."<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas also greatly influenced Catholic understandings of <a href="/wiki/Mortal_sin" title="Mortal sin">mortal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venial_sin" title="Venial sin">venial sins</a>.
</p><p>Thomas Aquinas refers to animals as dumb and that the natural order has declared animals for man's use. Thomas denied that human beings have any duty of charity to animals because they are not persons. Otherwise, it would be unlawful to kill them for food. But humans should still be charitable to them, for "cruel habits might carry over into our treatment of human beings."<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas contributed to <a href="/wiki/History_of_economic_thought#Middle_Ages" title="History of economic thought">economic thought</a> as an aspect of ethics and justice. He dealt with the concept of a <a href="/wiki/Just_price" title="Just price">just price</a>, normally its market price or a regulated price sufficient to cover seller <a href="/wiki/Costs_of_production" class="mw-redirect" title="Costs of production">costs of production</a>. He argued it was immoral for sellers to raise their prices simply because buyers were in pressing need for a product.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_order">Political order</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Political order">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Thomas's theory of political order became highly influential. He sees man as a social being that lives in a community and interacts with its other members. That leads, among other things, to the <a href="/wiki/Division_of_labour" title="Division of labour">division of labour</a>.
</p><p>Thomas made a distinction between a good man and a good citizen, which was important to the development of <a href="/wiki/Libertarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Libertarian">libertarian</a> theory. That is, the sphere of <a href="/wiki/Individual_autonomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Individual autonomy">individual autonomy</a> was one which the state could not interfere with.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas thinks that <a href="/wiki/Monarchy" title="Monarchy">monarchy</a> is the best form of government, because a monarch does not have to form compromises with other persons. Moreover, according to Thomas, <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchy</a> degenerates more easily into <a href="/wiki/Tyranny" class="mw-redirect" title="Tyranny">tyranny</a> than monarchy. To prevent a king from becoming a tyrant, his political powers must be curbed. Unless an agreement of all persons involved can be reached, a tyrant must be tolerated, as otherwise the political situation could deteriorate into <a href="/wiki/Anarchy" title="Anarchy">anarchy</a>, which would be even worse than tyranny.
</p><p>According to Thomas, monarchs are God's representatives in their territories, but the Church, represented by the popes, is above the kings in matters of doctrine and morality. As a consequence, worldly rulers are obliged to adapt their laws to the Catholic Church's doctrines and determinations.
</p><p>Following Aristotle's concept of <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>, Thomas justifies this institution on the grounds of <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychology">Psychology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Psychology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="box-Multiple_issues plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-multiple_issues compact-ambox" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span"><div class="mw-collapsible" style="width:95%; margin: 0.2em 0;"><b>This section has multiple issues.</b> Please help <b><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit">improve it</a></b> or discuss these issues on the <b><a href="/wiki/Talk:Thomas_Aquinas" title="Talk:Thomas Aquinas">talk page</a></b>. <small><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove these template messages</a>)</i></small>
<div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="margin-top: 0.3em;">
