<table class="box-Essay-like plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-essay-like" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>is written like a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not#Wikipedia_is_not_a_publisher_of_original_thought" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">personal reflection, personal essay, or argumentative essay</a></b> that states a Wikipedia editor's personal feelings or presents an original argument about a topic.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit">help improve it</a> by rewriting it in an <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Information_style_and_tone" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">encyclopedic style</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2013</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<table class="box-Expert_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs attention from an expert in Philosophy</b>. The specific problem is: <b>statements are not attributed or contextualised.</b><span class="hide-when-compact"> See the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Thomas_Aquinas#&quot;Psychology" title="Talk:Thomas Aquinas">talk page</a> for details. <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Philosophy" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Philosophy">WikiProject Philosophy</a> may be able to help recruit an expert.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2013</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
    </div>
</div><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Thomas Aquinas maintains that a human is a single material substance. He understands the soul as the form of the body, which makes a human being the composite of the two. Thus, only living, form-matter composites can truly be called human; dead bodies are "human" only analogously. One actually existing substance comes from body and soul. A human is a single material substance, but still should be understood as having an immaterial soul, which continues after bodily death.
</p><p>In his <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa theologiae</a></i> Thomas clearly states his position on the nature of the soul; defining it as "the first principle of life".<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The soul is not corporeal, or a body; it is the act of a body. Because the intellect is incorporeal, it does not use the bodily organs, as "the operation of anything follows the mode of its being."<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Thomas the soul is not matter, not even incorporeal or spiritual matter. If it were, it would not be able to understand universals, which are immaterial. A receiver receives things according to the receiver's own nature, so for soul (receiver) to understand (receive) universals, it must have the same nature as universals. Yet, any substance that understands universals may not be a matter-form composite. So, humans have rational souls, which are abstract forms independent of the body. But a human being is one existing, single material substance that comes from body and soul: that is what Thomas means when he writes that "something one in nature can be formed from an intellectual substance and a body", and "a thing one in nature does not result from two permanent entities unless one has the character of substantial form and the other of matter."<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The soul is a "<a href="/wiki/Substantial_form" title="Substantial form">substantial form</a>"; it is a part of a substance, but it is not a substance by itself. Nevertheless, the soul exists separately from the body, and continues, after death, in many of the capacities we think of as human. Substantial form is what makes a thing a member of the species to which it belongs, and substantial form is also the structure or configuration that provides the object with the abilities that make the object what it is. For humans, those abilities are those of the rational animal.
</p><p>These distinctions can be better understood in the light of Thomas's understanding of matter and form, a <a href="/wiki/Hylomorphism" title="Hylomorphism">hylomorphic</a> ("matter/form") theory derived from <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>. In any given substance, matter and form are necessarily united, and each is a necessary aspect of that substance. However, they are conceptually separable. Matter represents what is changeable about the substance‚Äîwhat is potentially something else. For example, bronze matter is potentially a statue, or also potentially a cymbal. Matter must be understood as the matter <i>of</i> something. In contrast, form is what determines some particular chunk of matter to be a specific substance and no other. When Thomas says that the human body is only partly composed of matter, he means the material body is only potentially a human being. The soul is what actualizes that potential into an existing human being. Consequently, the fact that a human body is live human tissue entails that a human soul is wholly present in each part of the human.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aquinas addressed most economic questions within the framework of justice, which he contended was the highest of virtues. He says that justice is "a habit whereby man renders to each his due by a constant and perpetual will."<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> He argued that this concept of justice has its roots in natural law. Joseph Schumpeter, in his <i>History of Economic Analysis</i>, concluded that "All the economic questions put together matters less to him than did the smallest point of theological or philosophical doctrine, and it is only where economic phenomena raise questions of moral theology that he touches upon them at all."<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Aquinas was careful to distinguish the <a href="/wiki/Just_price" title="Just price">just</a>, or natural, price of a good from that price which manipulates another party. He determines the just price from a number of things. First, the just price must be relative to the worth of the good. Aquinas holds that the price of a good measures its quality: "the quality of a thing that comes into human use is measured by the price given for it."<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> He goes on to say that the price of a good, measured by its worth, is determined by its usefulness to man. This worth is subjective because each good has a different level of usefulness to every man. Aquinas argues, then, that the price should reflect the current value of a good according to its usefulness to man. He continues: "Gold and silver are costly not only on account of the usefulness of the vessels and other like things made from them, but also on account of the excellence and purity of their substance."<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Aquinas also wrote extensively on <a href="/wiki/Usury" title="Usury">usury</a>, that is, the lending of money with interest. He condemned its practice: "to take usury for money lent is unjust in itself, because this is to sell what does not exist, and this evidently leads to inequality which is contrary to justice."<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Money, and other similar goods, are consumed only when they are used. Charging a premium for money lent is a charge for <i>more</i> than the use of the good. Thus, Aquinas concluded that the lender is charging for something not his own, in other words, not rendering to each his due.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Works_by_Thomas_Aquinas" class="mw-redirect" title="Works by Thomas Aquinas">Works by Thomas Aquinas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_von_Aquin_17th_century_sculpture.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Thomas_von_Aquin_17th_century_sculpture.jpeg/220px-Thomas_von_Aquin_17th_century_sculpture.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="279" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Thomas_von_Aquin_17th_century_sculpture.jpeg/330px-Thomas_von_Aquin_17th_century_sculpture.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Thomas_von_Aquin_17th_century_sculpture.jpeg/440px-Thomas_von_Aquin_17th_century_sculpture.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1195" data-file-height="1517" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_von_Aquin_17th_century_sculpture.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>17th-century sculpture of Thomas Aquinas</div></div></div>
<p>Thomas Aquinas viewed <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>, or the <i><a href="/wiki/Sacred" title="Sacred">sacred</a> <a href="/wiki/Doctrine" title="Doctrine">doctrine</a></i>, as a science,<sup id="cite_ref-Stanford_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> the raw material data of which consists of written <a href="/wiki/Sacred_Tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacred Tradition">scripture</a> and the tradition of the Catholic Church. These sources of data were produced by the self-revelation of God to individuals and groups of people throughout history. Faith and reason, while distinct but related, are the two primary tools for processing the data of theology. Thomas believed both were necessary‚Äîor, rather, that the <i>confluence</i> of both was necessary‚Äîfor one to obtain true knowledge of God. Thomas blended Greek philosophy and Christian doctrine by suggesting that rational thinking and the study of nature, like revelation, were valid ways to understand truths pertaining to God. According to Thomas, God reveals himself through nature, so to study nature is to study God. The ultimate goals of theology, in Thomas's mind, are to use reason to grasp the truth about God and to experience salvation through that truth. The central thought is <a href="/wiki/Gratia_non_tollit_naturam,_sed_perficit" title="Gratia non tollit naturam, sed perficit">Gratia non tollit naturam, sed perficit</a>. <a href="/wiki/Divine_grace" title="Divine grace">Grace</a> does not destroy nature, but perfects it.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revelation">Revelation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Revelation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Thomas believed that truth is known through reason (<a href="/wiki/Natural_revelation" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural revelation">natural revelation</a>) and faith (<a href="/wiki/Supernatural_revelation" class="mw-redirect" title="Supernatural revelation">supernatural revelation</a>). <i>Supernatural</i> revelation has its origin in the inspiration of the Holy Spirit and is made available through the teaching of the prophets, summed up in Holy Scripture, and transmitted by the <a href="/wiki/Magisterium" title="Magisterium">Magisterium</a>, the sum of which is called "Tradition". <i>Natural</i> revelation is the truth available to all people through their human nature and powers of reason. For example, he felt this applied to rational ways to know the existence of God.
</p><p>Though one may deduce the existence of God and his Attributes (Unity, Truth, Goodness, Power, Knowledge) through reason, certain specifics may be known only through the special revelation of God through <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a>. The major theological components of Christianity, such as the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Incarnation" title="Incarnation">Incarnation</a>, and charity are revealed in the teachings of the Church and the <a href="/wiki/Scripture" class="mw-redirect" title="Scripture">Scriptures</a> and may not otherwise be deduced.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Preserving_nature_within_grace">Preserving nature within grace</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Preserving nature within grace">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Revealed knowledge does not negate the truth and the completeness of human science as human, it further establishes them. First, it grants that the same things can be treated from two different perspectives without one canceling the other; thus there can be two sciences of God. Second, it provides the basis for the two sciences: one functions through the power of the light of natural reason, the other through the light of divine revelation. Moreover, they can, at least to some extent, keep out of each other's way because they differ "according to genus". Sacred doctrine is a fundamentally different kind of thing from theology, which is part of philosophy (ST I. 1.1 ad 2).
</p><p>Faith and reason complement rather than contradict each other, each giving different views of the same truth.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Creation">Creation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Creation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tommaso_-_Super_libros_de_generatione_et_corruptione_-_4733257_00007.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Tommaso_-_Super_libros_de_generatione_et_corruptione_-_4733257_00007.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Tommaso_-_Super_libros_de_generatione_et_corruptione_-_4733257_00007.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="318" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Tommaso_-_Super_libros_de_generatione_et_corruptione_-_4733257_00007.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Tommaso_-_Super_libros_de_generatione_et_corruptione_-_4733257_00007.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Tommaso_-_Super_libros_de_generatione_et_corruptione_-_4733257_00007.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Tommaso_-_Super_libros_de_generatione_et_corruptione_-_4733257_00007.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1680" data-file-height="2428" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tommaso_-_Super_libros_de_generatione_et_corruptione_-_4733257_00007.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Super libros de generatione et corruptione</i></div></div></div>
<p>As a Catholic Thomas believed that God is the "maker of heaven and earth, of all that is visible and invisible." Like Aristotle, Thomas posited that life could form from non-living material or plant life, a theory of ongoing <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">abiogenesis</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">spontaneous generation</a>:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Since the generation of one thing is the corruption of another, it was not incompatible with the first formation of things, that from the corruption of the less perfect the more perfect should be generated. Hence animals generated from the corruption of inanimate things, or of plants, may have been generated then.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tommaso_-_Super_Physicam_Aristotelis,_1595_-_4733624.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Tommaso_-_Super_Physicam_Aristotelis%2C_1595_-_4733624.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Tommaso_-_Super_Physicam_Aristotelis%2C_1595_-_4733624.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Tommaso_-_Super_Physicam_Aristotelis%2C_1595_-_4733624.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Tommaso_-_Super_Physicam_Aristotelis%2C_1595_-_4733624.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Tommaso_-_Super_Physicam_Aristotelis%2C_1595_-_4733624.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Tommaso_-_Super_Physicam_Aristotelis%2C_1595_-_4733624.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3481" data-file-height="4923" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tommaso_-_Super_Physicam_Aristotelis,_1595_-_4733624.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Super Physicam Aristotelis</i>, 1595</div></div></div>
<p>Additionally Thomas considered <a href="/wiki/Empedocles" title="Empedocles">Empedocles</a>'s theory that various mutated <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> emerged at the dawn of Creation. Thomas reasoned that these species were generated through <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutations</a> in animal <a href="/wiki/Sperm" title="Sperm">sperm</a>, and argued that they were not unintended by <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a>; rather, such species were simply not intended for perpetual existence. That discussion is found in his commentary on <i><a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)">Aristotle's Physics</a></i>:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The same thing is true of those substances Empedocles said were produced at the beginning of the world, such as the 'ox-progeny', i.e., half ox and half man. For if such things were not able to arrive at some end and final state of nature so that they would be preserved in existence, this was not because nature did not intend this [a final state], but because they were not capable of being preserved. For they were not generated according to nature, but by the corruption of some natural principle, as it now also happens that some monstrous offspring are generated because of the corruption of seed.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Just_war">Just war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Just war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Just_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Just War">Just War</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> agreed strongly with the conventional wisdom of his time, that Christians should be pacifists philosophically, but that they should use defense as a means of preserving peace in the long run. For example, he routinely argued that pacifism did not prevent the defence of innocents. In essence, the pursuit of peace might require fighting to preserve it in the long-term.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Such a war must not be preemptive, but defensive, to restore peace.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Clearly, some special characteristics sets apart "war" from "schism", "brawling", and "sedition". While it would be contradictory to speak of a "just schism", a "just brawling" or a "just sedition" (the three terms denote sin and sin only) "war" alone permits sub classification into good and bad kinds. Curiously, however, Augustine does not work up a terminological contrast between "just" and "unjust" war.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
Thomas Aquinas, centuries later, used the authority of Augustine's arguments in an attempt to define the conditions under which a war could be just.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> He laid these out in his historic work, Summa Theologica:
</p>
<ul><li>First, war must occur for a good and just purpose rather than the pursuit of wealth or power.</li>
<li>Second, just war must be waged by a properly instituted authority such as the state.</li>
<li>Third, peace must be a central motive even in the midst of violence.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="School_of_Salamanca">School of Salamanca</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: School of Salamanca">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some 200 years later, the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Salamanca" title="School of Salamanca">School of Salamanca</a> expanded Thomas's understanding of <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a> and just war. Given that war is one of the worst evils suffered by mankind, the adherents of the School reasoned that it ought to be resorted to only when it was necessary to prevent an even <i>greater</i> evil. A diplomatic agreement is preferable, even for the more powerful party, before a war is started.  Examples of "<a href="/wiki/The_Just_War_tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="The Just War tradition">just war</a>" are:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>In self-defense, as long as there is a reasonable possibility of success. If failure is a foregone conclusion, then it is just a wasteful spilling of blood.</li>
<li>Preventive war against a <a href="/wiki/Tyranny" class="mw-redirect" title="Tyranny">tyrant</a> who is about to attack.</li>
<li>War to punish a guilty enemy.</li></ul>
<p>A war is not legitimate or illegitimate simply based on its original motivation: it must comply with a series of additional requirements:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The response must be commensurate with the evil; more violence than is strictly necessary would be unjust.</li>
<li>Governing authorities <i>declare</i> war, but their decision is not sufficient cause to begin a war. If the <i>people</i> oppose a war, then it is illegitimate. The people have a right to depose a government that is waging, or is about to wage, an unjust war.</li>
<li>Once war has begun, there remain moral limits to action. For example, one may not attack innocents or kill hostages.</li>
<li>The belligerents must exhaust all options for dialogue and negotiation before undertaking a war; war is legitimate only as a last resort.</li></ul>
<p>Under this doctrine, expansionist wars, wars of pillage, wars to convert <a href="/wiki/Infidel" title="Infidel">infidels</a> or <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagans</a>, and wars for glory are all inherently unjust.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_of_God">Nature of God</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Nature of God">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Thomas believed that the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a> is self-evident in itself, but not to us. "Therefore I say that this proposition, "God exists", of itself is self-evident, for the predicate is the same as the subject<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... Now because we do not know the essence of God, the proposition is not self-evident to us; but needs to be demonstrated by things that are more known to us, though less known in their nature‚Äînamely, by effects."<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas believed that the existence of God can be demonstrated. Briefly in the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa theologiae</a></i> and more extensively in the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_contra_Gentiles" title="Summa contra Gentiles">Summa contra Gentiles</a></i>, he considered in great detail five arguments for the existence of God, widely known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Quinque_viae" class="mw-redirect" title="Quinque viae">quinque viae</a></i> (Five Ways).
</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For detailed analysis of the five proofs, see <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">Existence of God</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the original text of the five proofs, see <a href="/wiki/Quinque_viae" class="mw-redirect" title="Quinque viae">Quinque viae</a></div>
<ol><li>Motion: Some things undoubtedly move, though cannot cause their own motion. Since, as Thomas believed, there can be no infinite chain of causes of motion, there must be a <a href="/wiki/Unmoved_mover" title="Unmoved mover">First Mover</a> not moved by anything else, and this is what everyone understands by God.</li>
<li class="mw-empty-elt"></li>
<li>Causation: As in the case of motion, nothing can cause itself, and an infinite chain of causation is impossible, so there must be a <a href="/wiki/Prima_causa" class="mw-redirect" title="Prima causa">First Cause</a>, called God.</li>
<li class="mw-empty-elt"></li>
<li>Existence of necessary and the unnecessary: Our experience includes things certainly existing but apparently unnecessary. Not everything can be unnecessary, for then once there was nothing and there would still be nothing. Therefore, we are compelled to suppose something that exists necessarily, having this necessity only from itself; in fact itself the cause for other things to exist.</li>
<li class="mw-empty-elt"></li>
<li>Gradation: If we can notice a gradation in things in the sense that some things are more hot, good, etc., there must be a superlative that is the truest and noblest thing, and so most fully existing. <span title="Note that Thomas does not ascribe actual qualities to God Himself." class="rt-commentedText" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">This then, we call God</span></li>
<li class="mw-empty-elt"></li>
<li>Ordered tendencies of nature: A direction of actions to an end is noticed in all bodies following natural laws. Anything without awareness tends to a goal under the guidance of one who is aware. <span title="Note that even when we guide objects, in Thomas&#39;s view the source of all our knowledge comes from God as well." class="rt-commentedText" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">This we call God</span><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Concerning the nature of God, Thomas felt the best approach, commonly called the <i><a href="/wiki/Negative_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Negative theology">via negativa</a></i>, is to consider what God is not. This led him to propose five statements about the divine qualities:
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Divine_simplicity" title="Divine simplicity">God is simple</a>, without composition of parts, such as body and soul, or matter and form.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>God is perfect, lacking nothing. That is, God is distinguished from other beings on account of God's complete actuality.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Thomas defined God as the '<i>Ipse <span class="plainlinks"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://aquinasactusessendi.blogspot.com">Actus Essendi</a></span> subsistens</i>,' subsisting act of being.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>God is infinite. That is, God is not finite in the ways that created beings are physically, intellectually, and emotionally limited. This infinity is to be distinguished from infinity of size and infinity of number.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>God is immutable, incapable of change on the levels of God's essence and character.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>God is one, without diversification within God's self. The unity of God is such that God's essence is the same as God's existence. In Thomas's words, "in itself the proposition 'God exists' is <a href="/wiki/Logical_truth" title="Logical truth">necessarily true</a>, for in it subject and predicate are the same."<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_of_Sin">Nature of Sin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Nature of Sin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following <a href="/wiki/St._Augustine_of_Hippo" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Augustine of Hippo">St. Augustine of Hippo</a>, Thomas defines <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">sin</a> as "a word, deed, or desire, contrary to the <a href="/wiki/Divine_law" title="Divine law">eternal law</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> It is important to note the analogous nature of law in Thomas's legal philosophy. Natural law is an instance or instantiation of eternal law. Because natural law is what human beings determine according to their own nature (as rational beings), disobeying reason is disobeying natural law and eternal law. Thus eternal law is logically prior to reception of either "natural law" (that determined by reason) or "divine law" (that found in the Old and New Testaments). In other words, God's will extends to both reason and revelation. Sin is abrogating either one's own reason, on the one hand, or revelation on the other, and is synonymous with "evil" (<a href="/wiki/Privation" title="Privation">privation</a> of good, or <i><a href="/wiki/Privatio_boni" class="mw-redirect" title="Privatio boni">privatio boni</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>). Thomas, like all Scholastics, generally argued that the findings of reason and data of revelation cannot conflict, so both are a guide to God's will for human beings.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_of_the_Trinity">Nature of the Trinity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Nature of the Trinity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Thomas argued that God, while perfectly united, also is perfectly described by <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Three Interrelated Persons</a>. These three persons (Father, Son, and Holy Spirit) are constituted by their relations within the essence of God. Thomas wrote that the term "Trinity" "does not mean the relations themselves of the Persons, but rather the number of persons related to each other; and hence it is that the word in itself does not express regard to another."<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> The Father generates the Son (or the Word) by the relation of self-awareness. This eternal generation then produces an eternal Spirit "who enjoys the divine nature as the Love of God, the Love of the Father for the Word."
</p><p>This Trinity exists independently from the world. It transcends the created world, but the Trinity also decided to give grace to human beings. This takes place through the <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">Incarnation</a> of the Word in the person of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a> and through the indwelling of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> within those who have experienced <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> by God; according to Aidan Nichols.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Prima_causa_.28first_cause.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Prima_causa_(first_cause)"><i>Prima causa</i> (first cause)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Prima causa (first cause)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Thomas's five proofs for the existence of God take some of Aristotle's assertions concerning principles of being. For God as <i><a href="/wiki/Prima_causa" class="mw-redirect" title="Prima causa">prima causa</a></i> ("first cause") comes from Aristotle's concept of the <a href="/wiki/Unmoved_mover" title="Unmoved mover">unmoved mover</a> and asserts that God is the ultimate cause of all things.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_of_Jesus_Christ">Nature of Jesus Christ</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Nature of Jesus Christ">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the <i>Summa Theologica</i> Thomas begins his discussion of Jesus Christ by recounting the biblical story of <a href="/wiki/Adam_and_Eve" title="Adam and Eve">Adam and Eve</a> and by describing the negative effects of <a href="/wiki/Original_sin" title="Original sin">original sin</a>. The purpose of Christ's Incarnation was to restore human nature by removing <i>the contamination of sin</i>, which humans cannot do by themselves. "Divine Wisdom judged it fitting that God should become man, so that thus one and the same person would be able both to restore man and to offer satisfaction."<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Thomas argued in favor of the <a href="/wiki/Atonement_(satisfaction_view)" class="mw-redirect" title="Atonement (satisfaction view)">satisfaction view of atonement</a>; that is, that <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_of_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian views of Jesus">Jesus Christ</a> <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Death of Jesus">died</a> "to satisfy for the whole human race, which was sentenced to die on account of sin."<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas argued against several specific contemporary and historical theologians who held differing views about Christ. In response to <a href="/wiki/Photinus" title="Photinus">Photinus</a>, Thomas stated that Jesus was truly divine and not simply a human being. Against <a href="/wiki/Nestorius" title="Nestorius">Nestorius</a>, who suggested that Son of God was merely conjoined to the man Christ, Thomas argued that the fullness of God was an integral part of Christ's existence. However, countering <a href="/wiki/Apollinaris_of_Laodicea" title="Apollinaris of Laodicea">Apollinaris</a>' views, Thomas held that Christ had a truly human (rational) <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a>, as well. This produced a duality of natures in Christ. Thomas argued against <a href="/wiki/Eutyches" title="Eutyches">Eutyches</a> that this duality persisted after the Incarnation. Thomas stated that these two natures existed simultaneously yet distinguishably in one real human body, unlike the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Manichaeus" class="mw-redirect" title="Manichaeus">Manichaeus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Valentinus_(Gnostic)" title="Valentinus (Gnostic)">Valentinus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With respect to <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul</a>'s assertion that Christ, "though he was in the form of God<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... emptied himself" (<a href="/wiki/Philippians" class="mw-redirect" title="Philippians">Philippians</a> 2:6‚Äì7) in becoming human, Thomas offered an articulation of divine <a href="/wiki/Kenosis" title="Kenosis">kenosis</a> that has informed much subsequent Catholic <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christology</a>. Following the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">Council of Nicaea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>, as well as the assertions of Scripture, Thomas held the doctrine of divine immutability.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Hence, in becoming human, there could be no change in the divine person of Christ. For Thomas, "the mystery of Incarnation was not completed through God being changed in any way from the state in which He had been from eternity, but through His having united Himself to the creature in a new way, or rather through having united it to Himself."<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, Thomas explained that Christ "emptied Himself, not by putting off His divine nature, but by assuming a human nature."<sup id="cite_ref-dhspriory.org_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dhspriory.org-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> For Thomas, "the divine nature is sufficiently full, because every perfection of goodness is there. But human nature and the soul are not full, but capable of fulness, because it was made as a slate not written upon. Therefore, human nature is empty."<sup id="cite_ref-dhspriory.org_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dhspriory.org-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Thus, when Paul indicates that Christ "emptied himself" this is to be understood in light of his assumption of a human nature.
</p><p>In short "Christ had a <i>real body</i> of the same nature of ours, a <i>true rational soul</i>, and, together with these, <i>perfect Deity</i>". Thus, there is both unity (in his one <i><a href="/wiki/Hypostasis_(philosophy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypostasis (philosophy)">hypostasis</a></i>) and composition (in his two natures, human and Divine) in Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I answer that, The Person or hypostasis of Christ may be viewed in two ways. First as it is in itself, and thus it is altogether simple, even as the Nature of the Word. Secondly, in the aspect of person or hypostasis to which it belongs to subsist in a nature; and thus the Person of Christ subsists in two natures. Hence though there is one subsisting being in Him, yet there are different aspects of subsistence, and hence He is said to be a composite person, insomuch as one being subsists in two.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Echoing <a href="/wiki/Athanasius_of_Alexandria" title="Athanasius of Alexandria">Athanasius of Alexandria</a>, he said that "The only begotten Son of God<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... assumed our nature, so that he, made man, might make men gods."<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Goal_of_human_life">Goal of human life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Goal of human life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Thomas Aquinas identified the goal of human existence as union and eternal fellowship with God. This goal is achieved through the <a href="/wiki/Beatific_vision" title="Beatific vision">beatific vision</a>, in which a person experiences perfect, unending happiness by seeing the essence of God. The vision occurs after death as a gift from God to those who in life experienced salvation and redemption through Christ.
</p><p>The goal of union with God has implications for the individual's life on earth. Thomas stated that an individual's <a href="/wiki/Free_Will" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Will">will</a> must be ordered toward right things, such as charity, peace, and <a href="/wiki/Sacred" title="Sacred">holiness</a>. He saw this orientation as also the way to happiness. Indeed, Thomas ordered his treatment of the moral life around the idea of happiness. The relationship between will and goal is antecedent in nature "because rectitude of the will consists in being duly ordered to the last end [that is, the beatific vision]." Those who truly seek to understand and see God will necessarily love what God loves. Such love requires morality and bears fruit in everyday human choices.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment_of_heretics">Treatment of heretics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Treatment of heretics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p>
Thomas Aquinas belonged to the Dominican Order (formally <i><a href="/wiki/Ordo_Praedicatorum" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordo Praedicatorum">Ordo Praedicatorum</a></i>, the Order of Preachers) who began as an order dedicated to the conversion of the <a href="/wiki/Albigensians" class="mw-redirect" title="Albigensians">Albigensians</a> and other heterodox factions, at first by peaceful means; later the Albigensians were dealt with by means of the <a href="/wiki/Albigensian_Crusade" title="Albigensian Crusade">Albigensian Crusade</a>. In the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa theologiae</a></i>, he wrote: </p><blockquote><p>With regard to heretics two points must be observed: one, on their own side; the other, on the side of the Church. On their own side there is the sin, whereby they deserve not only to be separated from the Church by excommunication, but also to be severed from the world by death. For it is a much graver matter to corrupt the faith that quickens the soul, than to forge money, which supports temporal life. Wherefore if forgers of money and other evil-doers are forthwith condemned to death by the secular authority, much more reason is there for heretics, as soon as they are convicted of heresy, to be not only excommunicated but even put to death.
On the part of the Church, however, there is mercy, which looks to the conversion of the wanderer, wherefore she condemns not at once, but "after the first and second admonition", as <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">the Apostle</a> directs: after that, if he is yet stubborn, the Church no longer hoping for his conversion, looks to the salvation of others, by excommunicating him and separating him from the Church, and furthermore delivers him to the secular tribunal to be exterminated thereby from the world by death.(<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.newadvent.org/summa/3011.htm#article3"><i>Summa</i>, II‚ÄìII, Q.11, art.3.</a>)</p></blockquote>
<p>Heresy was a capital offense against the secular law of most European countries of the 13th century, which had a limited prison capacity. Kings and emperors, even those at war with the papacy, listed heresy first among the crimes against the state. Kings claimed power from God according to the Christian faith. Often enough, especially in that age of papal claims to universal worldly power, the rulers' power was tangibly and visibly legitimated directly through coronation by the pope.
</p><p>
Simple theft, forgery, fraud, and other such crimes were also capital offenses; Thomas's point seems to be that the gravity of this offense, which touches not only the material goods but also the spiritual goods of others, is at least the same as forgery. Thomas's suggestion specifically demands that heretics be handed to a "secular tribunal" rather than <a href="/wiki/Magisterium" title="Magisterium">magisterial</a> authority. That Thomas specifically says that heretics "deserve<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... death" is related to his theology, according to which all sinners have no intrinsic right to life ("For the wages of sin is death; but the free gift of God is eternal life in Christ Jesus our Lord"<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>). Although the life of a heretic who repents should be spared, the former heretic should be executed if he relapses into heresy. Thomas elaborates on his opinion regarding heresy in the next article, when he says:</p><blockquote><p>In God's tribunal, those who return are always received, because God is a searcher of hearts, and knows those who return in sincerity. But the Church cannot imitate God in this, for she presumes that those who relapse after being once received, are not sincere in their return; hence she does not debar them from the way of salvation, but neither does she protect them from the sentence of death.
For this reason the Church not only admits to Penance those who return from heresy for the first time, but also safeguards their lives, and sometimes by dispensation, restores them to the ecclesiastical dignities which they may have had before, should their conversion appear to be sincere: we read of this as having frequently been done for the good of peace. But when they fall again, after having been received, this seems to prove them to be inconstant in faith, wherefore when they return again, they are admitted to Penance, but are not delivered from the pain of death.(<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.newadvent.org/summa/3011.htm#article4"><i>Summa</i>, op. cit., art.4.</a>)</p></blockquote>
<p>For <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>, Thomas argues for toleration of both their persons and their religious rites.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magic_and_its_practitioners">Magic and its practitioners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Magic and its practitioners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Regarding magic, Aquinas wrote that
</p>
<ul><li>only God can perform miracles, create and transform (<i><a href="/wiki/Summa_contra_gentiles" class="mw-redirect" title="Summa contra gentiles">Summa contra gentiles</a></i> 102),</li>
<li>angels and demons ("spiritual substances") may do wonderful things, but they are not miracles and merely use natural things as instruments (ibid.,103),</li>
<li>any efficacy of magicians does not come from the power of particular words, or celestial bodies, or special figures, or sympathetic magic, but by bidding (ibid.,105),</li>
<li>"demons" are intellective substances who were created good and have chosen to be bad, it is these who are bid.(ibid.,106-108),</li>
<li>if there is some transformation that could not occur in nature it is either the demon working on human imagination or arranging a fake (<i><a href="/wiki/Summa_theologica" class="mw-redirect" title="Summa theologica">Summa theologica</a></i> I, 114, 4).</li></ul>
<p>A mention of witchcraft appears in the <a href="/wiki/Summa_theologica" class="mw-redirect" title="Summa theologica">Summa theologicae</a><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> and concludes that the Church does not treat temporary or permanent impotence attributed to a spell any differently to that of natural causes, as far as an impediment to marriage.
</p><p>Under the <i><a href="/wiki/Canon_Episcopi" title="Canon Episcopi">canon Episcopi</a></i>, church doctrine held that witchcraft was not possible and any practitioners of sorcery were deluded and their acts an illusion. Thomas Aquinas was instrumental in developing a new doctrine that included the belief in the real power of witches<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Disputed_statement" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Disputed statement"><span title="This claim has reliable sources with contradicting facts (August 2018)">disputed</span></a>&#32;<span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Thomas_Aquinas" title="Talk:Thomas Aquinas">discuss</a></span></i>&#93;</sup>. This was a departure from the teachings of his master <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a> whose doctrine was based in the <i>Episcopi</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The famous fifteenth century witch-hunter's manual, the <i><a href="/wiki/Malleus_Maleficarum" title="Malleus Maleficarum">Malleus Maleficarum</a></i>, also written by a member of the Dominican Order, begins by quoting Aquinas ("Commentary on Pronouncements" Sent.4.34.I.Co.) refuting<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Disputed_statement" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Disputed statement"><span title="This claim has reliable sources with contradicting facts (August 2018)">disputed</span></a>&#32;<span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Thomas_Aquinas" title="Talk:Thomas Aquinas">discuss</a></span></i>&#93;</sup> the <i>Episcopi</i> and goes on to cite Aquinas over a hundred times.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Promoters of the witch phobia that followed often quoted Aquinas more than any other source.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thoughts_on_afterlife_and_resurrection">Thoughts on afterlife and resurrection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Thoughts on afterlife and resurrection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A grasp of Thomas's psychology is essential for understanding his beliefs around the afterlife and resurrection. Thomas, following Church doctrine, accepts that the soul continues to exist after the death of the body. Because he accepts that the soul is the form of the body, then he also must believe that the human being, like all material things, is form-matter composite. Substantial form (the human soul) configures prime matter (the physical body) and is the form by which a material composite belongs to that species it does; in the case of human beings, that species is rational animal.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> So, a human being is a matter-form composite that is organized to be a rational animal. Matter cannot exist without being configured by form, but form can exist without matter‚Äîwhich allows for the separation of soul from body. Thomas says that the soul shares in the material and spiritual worlds, and so has some features of matter and other, immaterial, features (such as access to universals). The human soul is different from other material and spiritual things; it is created by God, but also comes into existence only in the material body.
</p><p>Human beings are material, but the human person can survive the death of the body through continued existence of the soul, which persists. The human soul straddles the spiritual and material worlds, and is both a configured subsistent form as well as a configurer of matter into that of a living, bodily human.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Because it is spiritual, the human soul does not depend on matter and may exist separately. Because the human being is a soul-matter composite, the body has a part in what it is to be human. Perfected human nature consists in the human dual nature, embodied and intellecting.
</p><p>Resurrection appears to require dualism, which Thomas rejects. Yet Thomas believes the soul persists after the death and corruption of the body, and is capable of existence, separated from the body between the time of death and the resurrection. Thomas believes in a different sort of dualism, one guided by Christian scripture. Thomas knows that human beings are essentially physical, but physicality has a spirit capable of returning to God after life.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> For Thomas, the rewards and punishment of the afterlife are not <i>only</i> spiritual. Because of this, resurrection is an important part of his philosophy on the soul. The human is fulfilled and complete in the body, so the hereafter must take place with souls enmattered in resurrected bodies. In addition to spiritual reward, humans can expect to enjoy material and physical blessings. Because Thomas's soul requires a body for its actions, during the afterlife, the soul will also be punished or rewarded in corporeal existence.
</p><p>Thomas states clearly his stance on resurrection, and uses it to back up his philosophy of justice; that is, the promise of resurrection compensates Christians who suffered in this world through a heavenly union with the divine. He says, "If there is no resurrection of the dead, it follows that there is no good for human beings other than in this life."<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Resurrection provides the impetus for people on earth to give up pleasures in this life. Aquinas believes the human who prepared for the afterlife both morally and intellectually will be rewarded more greatly; however, all reward is through the grace of God. Thomas insists beatitude will be conferred according to merit, and will render the person better able to conceive the divine. Thomas accordingly believes punishment is directly related to earthly, living preparation and activity as well. Thomas's account of the soul focuses on epistemology and metaphysics, and because of this he believes it gives a clear account of the immaterial nature of the soul. Thomas conservatively guards Christian doctrine, and thus maintains physical and spiritual reward and punishment after death. By accepting the essentiality of both body and soul, he allows for a heaven and hell described in scripture and church dogma.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_influence">Modern influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Modern influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Joseph%27s_Catholic_Church_(Central_City,_Kentucky)_-_stained_glass,_St._Thomas_Aquinas,_detail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Saint_Joseph%27s_Catholic_Church_%28Central_City%2C_Kentucky%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg/170px-Saint_Joseph%27s_Catholic_Church_%28Central_City%2C_Kentucky%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Saint_Joseph%27s_Catholic_Church_%28Central_City%2C_Kentucky%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg/255px-Saint_Joseph%27s_Catholic_Church_%28Central_City%2C_Kentucky%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Saint_Joseph%27s_Catholic_Church_%28Central_City%2C_Kentucky%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg/340px-Saint_Joseph%27s_Catholic_Church_%28Central_City%2C_Kentucky%29_-_stained_glass%2C_St._Thomas_Aquinas%2C_detail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2848" data-file-height="4288" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saint_Joseph%27s_Catholic_Church_(Central_City,_Kentucky)_-_stained_glass,_St._Thomas_Aquinas,_detail.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A stained glass window of Thomas Aquinas in St. Joseph's Catholic Church (<a href="/wiki/Central_City,_Kentucky" title="Central City, Kentucky">Central City, Kentucky</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>Many modern ethicists both within and outside the Catholic Church (notably <a href="/wiki/Philippa_Foot" title="Philippa Foot">Philippa Foot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alasdair_MacIntyre" title="Alasdair MacIntyre">Alasdair MacIntyre</a>) have recently commented on the possible use of Thomas's <a href="/wiki/Virtue_ethics" title="Virtue ethics">virtue ethics</a> as a way of avoiding <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a> or Kantian "sense of duty" (called <a href="/wiki/Deontology" class="mw-redirect" title="Deontology">deontology</a>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Through the work of twentieth-century philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/G._E._M._Anscombe" title="G. E. M. Anscombe">Elizabeth Anscombe</a> (especially in her book <i>Intention</i>), Thomas's <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_double_effect" title="Principle of double effect">principle of double effect</a> specifically and his theory of intentional activity generally have been influential.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In recent years the cognitive neuroscientist <a href="/wiki/Walter_J._Freeman_(neuroscientist)" class="mw-redirect" title="Walter J. Freeman (neuroscientist)">Walter Freeman</a> proposes that Thomism is the philosophical system explaining cognition that is most compatible with <a href="/wiki/Neurodynamics" class="mw-redirect" title="Neurodynamics">neurodynamics</a>, in a 2008 article in the journal <i>Mind and Matter</i> titled "Nonlinear Brain Dynamics and Intention According to Aquinas".
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Henry_Adams" title="Henry Adams">Henry Adams</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Mont_Saint_Michel_and_Chartres" title="Mont Saint Michel and Chartres">Mont Saint Michel and Chartres</a></i> ends with a culminating chapter on Thomas, in which Adams calls Thomas an "artist" and constructs an extensive analogy between the design of Thomas's "Church Intellectual" and that of the gothic cathedrals of that period. <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Panofsky" title="Erwin Panofsky">Erwin Panofsky</a> later would echo these views in <i>Gothic Architecture and Scholasticism</i> (1951).
</p><p>Thomas's aesthetic theories, especially the concept of <i>claritas</i>, deeply influenced the literary practice of modernist writer <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">James Joyce</a>, who used to extol Thomas as being second only to Aristotle among Western philosophers. Joyce refers to Thomas's doctrines in <i>Elementa philosophiae ad mentem D. Thomae Aquinatis doctoris angelici</i> (1898) of Girolamo Maria Mancini, professor of theology at the <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_University_of_Saint_Thomas_Aquinas" title="Pontifical University of Saint Thomas Aquinas">Collegium Divi Thomae de Urbe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> For example, Mancini's <i>Elementa</i> is referred to in Joyce's <i><a href="/wiki/Portrait_of_the_Artist_as_a_Young_Man" class="mw-redirect" title="Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man">Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The influence of Thomas's aesthetics also can be found in the works of the Italian <a href="/wiki/Semiotics" title="Semiotics">semiotician</a> <a href="/wiki/Umberto_Eco" title="Umberto Eco">Umberto Eco</a>, who wrote an essay on aesthetic ideas in Thomas (published in 1956 and republished in 1988 in a revised edition).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Russell.27s_criticism_of_Thomas_as_philosopher"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Russell's_criticism_of_Thomas_as_philosopher">Russell's criticism of Thomas as philosopher</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Russell&#039;s criticism of Thomas as philosopher">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-POV plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-POV" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Unbalanced scales.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/45px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png" decoding="async" width="45" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/68px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/90px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="354" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">The <b><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">neutrality</a> of this section is <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NPOV_dispute" title="Wikipedia:NPOV dispute">disputed</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Relevant discussion may be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Thomas_Aquinas##" title="Talk:Thomas Aquinas">talk page</a>. Please do not remove this message until <a href="/wiki/Template:POV#When_to_remove" title="Template:POV">conditions to do so are met</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2019</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Twentieth century philosopher <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a> criticized Thomas's philosophy stating that,
</p>
<blockquote><p>He does not, like the Platonic Socrates, set out to follow wherever the argument may lead. He is not engaged in an inquiry, the result of which it is impossible to know in advance. Before he begins to philosophize, he already knows the truth; it is declared in the Catholic faith. If he can find apparently rational arguments for some parts of the faith, so much the better; if he cannot, he need only fall back on revelation. The finding of arguments for a conclusion given in advance is not philosophy, but special pleading. I cannot, therefore, feel that he deserves to be put on a level with the best philosophers either of Greece or of modern times.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>This criticism is illustrated with the following example: According to Russell, Thomas advocates the indissolubility of marriage "on the ground that the father is useful in the education of the children, (a) because he is more rational than the mother, (b) because, being stronger, he is better able to inflict physical punishment."<sup id="cite_ref-Russell_1967_462_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russell_1967_462-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Even though modern approaches to education do not support these views, "No follower of Saint Thomas would, on that account, cease to believe in lifelong monogamy, because the real grounds of belief are not those which are alleged."<sup id="cite_ref-Russell_1967_462_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russell_1967_462-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> It may be countered that the treatment of matrimony in the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa Theologica</a></i> is in the Supplements volume, which was not written by Thomas.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, as noted above,<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Thomas's introduction of arguments and concepts from the pagan <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> and Muslim <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a> was controversial within the Catholic Church of his day.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Anthony_Kenny" title="Anthony Kenny">Anthony Kenny</a> suggests that Russell is failing to reflect on what philosophers, himself included, actually do:
</p>
<blockquote><p>It is extraordinary that that accusation should be made by Russell, who in the book <i>Principia Mathematica</i> takes hundreds of pages to prove that two and two make four, which is something he had believed all his life.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Thomas_Aquinas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_works_by_Thomas_Aquinas" title="List of works by Thomas Aquinas">List of works by Thomas Aquinas</a></div>
<p>The first edition of Thomas's <a href="/wiki/The_Complete_Works" title="The Complete Works">opera omnia</a>, the so-called <i>editio Piana</i> (from <a href="/wiki/Pius_V" class="mw-redirect" title="Pius V">Pius V</a>, the Dominican Pope who commissioned it), was produced in 1570 at the <i>studium</i> of the Roman convent at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_sopra_Minerva" title="Santa Maria sopra Minerva">Santa Maria sopra Minerva</a>, the forerunner of the <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_University_of_Saint_Thomas_Aquinas" title="Pontifical University of Saint Thomas Aquinas">Pontifical University of Saint Thomas Aquinas, <i>Angelicum</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The critical edition of Thomas's works is the ongoing edition commissioned by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_XIII" title="Pope Leo XIII">Pope Leo XIII</a> (1882‚Äì1903), the so-called <a href="/wiki/Leonine_Edition" class="mw-redirect" title="Leonine Edition">Leonine Edition</a>.
Most of his major works have now been edited, the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologiae" class="mw-redirect" title="Summa Theologiae">Summa Theologiae</a></i> in nine volumes during 1888‚Äì1906, the <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_contra_Gentiles" title="Summa contra Gentiles">Summa contra Gentiles</a></i> in three volumes during 1918‚Äì1930.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Abb%C3%A9_Migne" class="mw-redirect" title="Abb√© Migne">Abb√© Migne</a> published an edition of the <i>Summa Theologiae</i>, in four volumes, as an appendix to his <i><a href="/wiki/Patrologiae_Cursus_Completus" class="mw-redirect" title="Patrologiae Cursus Completus">Patrologiae Cursus Completus</a></i> (English editions: <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Rickaby" title="Joseph Rickaby">Joseph Rickaby</a> 1872, J.M. Ashley 1888).
</p><p>Electronic texts of mostly the Leonine Edition are maintained online by the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.corpusthomisticum.org/">Corpus Thomisticum</a> (corpusthomisticum.org) by Enrique Alarc√≥n, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Navarra" title="University of Navarra">University of Navarra</a>, and at <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.documentacatholicaomnia.eu/20_50_1225-1274-_Thomas_Aquinas,_Sanctus.html">Documenta Catholica Omnia</a>.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See Pius XI, <i>Studiorum Ducem</i> 11 (29 June 1923), AAS, XV ("non modo Angelicum, sed etiam Communem seu Universalem Ecclesiae Doctorem"). The title <i>Doctor Communis</i> dates to the fourteenth century; the title <i>Doctor Angelicus</i> dates to the fifteenth century, see Walz, <i>Xenia Thomistica</i>, III, p. 164 n. 4. <a href="/wiki/Tolomeo_da_Lucca" class="mw-redirect" title="Tolomeo da Lucca">Tolomeo da Lucca</a> writes in <i>Historia Ecclesiastica</i> (1317): "This man is supreme among modern teachers of philosophy and theology, and indeed in every subject. And such is the common view and opinion, so that nowadays in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a> they call him the <i>Doctor Communis</i> because of the outstanding clarity of his teaching." <i>Historia Eccles.</i> xxiii, c. 9.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Compendium Historiae Ordinis Praedicatorum, A.M. Walz, Herder 1930, 214: Romanus conventus S. Mariae supra Minervam anno 1255 ex conditionibus parvis crevit. Tunc enim paenitentibus feminis in communi regulariter ibi 1252/53 viventibus ad S. Pancratium migratis fratres Praedicatores domum illam relictam a Summo Pontifice habendam petierunt et impetranint. Qua demum feliciter obtenda capellam hospitio circa annum 1255 adiecerunt. Huc evangelizandi causa fratres e conventu S. Sabinae descendebant. <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="https://archive.org/stream/MN5081ucmf_3/MN5081ucmf_3_djvu.txt">https://archive.org/stream/MN5081ucmf_3/MN5081ucmf_3_djvu.txt</a> Accessed 5-17-2011</span>
